The **tiger** (***Panthera tigris***) is a large [cat](/wiki/Felidae "Felidae") and a member of the genus *[Panthera](/wiki/Panthera "Panthera")* native to [Asia](/wiki/Asia "Asia"). It has a powerful, muscular body with a large head and paws, a long tail and orange fur with black, mostly vertical stripes. It is traditionally classified into nine [recent](/wiki/Holocene "Holocene") [subspecies](/wiki/Subspecies "Subspecies"), though some recognise only two subspecies, mainland Asian tigers and the island tigers of the [Sunda Islands](/wiki/Sunda_Islands "Sunda Islands").

Throughout the tiger's range, it inhabits mainly [forests](/wiki/Forest "Forest"), from [coniferous](/wiki/Conifer "Conifer") and [temperate broadleaf and mixed forests](/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forest "Temperate broadleaf and mixed forest") in the [Russian Far East](/wiki/Russian_Far_East "Russian Far East") and [Northeast China](/wiki/Northeast_China "Northeast China") to [tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests](/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests "Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests") on the [Indian subcontinent](/wiki/Indian_subcontinent "Indian subcontinent") and [Southeast Asia](/wiki/Southeast_Asia "Southeast Asia"). The tiger is an [apex predator](/wiki/Apex_predator "Apex predator") and preys mainly on [ungulates](/wiki/Ungulate "Ungulate"), which it takes by ambush. It lives a mostly solitary life and occupies [home ranges](/wiki/Home_range "Home range"), defending these from individuals of the same sex. The range of a male tiger overlaps with that of multiple females with whom he mates. Females give birth to usually two or three cubs that stay with their mother for about two years. When becoming independent, they leave their mother's home range and establish their own.

Since the early 20th century, tiger populations have lost at least 93% of their historic range and are [locally extinct](/wiki/Local_extinction "Local extinction") in [West](/wiki/West_Asia "West Asia") and [Central Asia](/wiki/Central_Asia "Central Asia"), in large areas of [China](/wiki/China "China") and on the islands of [Java](/wiki/Java "Java") and [Bali](/wiki/Bali "Bali"). Today, the tiger's range is severely fragmented. It is listed as [Endangered](/wiki/Endangered_species_(IUCN_status) "Endangered species (IUCN status)") on the [IUCN Red List of Threatened Species](/wiki/IUCN_Red_List_of_Threatened_Species "IUCN Red List of Threatened Species"), as its range is thought to have declined by 53% to 68% since the late 1990s. Major threats to tigers are [habitat destruction](/wiki/Habitat_destruction "Habitat destruction") and [fragmentation](/wiki/Habitat_fragmentation "Habitat fragmentation") due to [deforestation](/wiki/Deforestation "Deforestation"), [poaching](/wiki/Poaching "Poaching") for fur and the illegal trade of body parts for medicinal purposes. Tigers are also victims of [humanâ€“wildlife conflict](/wiki/Human%E2%80%93wildlife_conflict "Humanâ€“wildlife conflict") as they attack and prey on livestock in areas where natural prey is scarce. The tiger is legally protected in all range countries. National conservation measures consist of action plans, [anti-poaching](/wiki/Anti-poaching "Anti-poaching") patrols and schemes for monitoring tiger populations. In several range countries, [wildlife corridors](/wiki/Wildlife_corridor "Wildlife corridor") have been established and tiger reintroduction is planned.

The tiger is among the most popular of the world's [charismatic megafauna](/wiki/Charismatic_megafauna "Charismatic megafauna"). It has been kept in captivity since ancient times and has been trained to perform in [circuses](/wiki/Circus "Circus") and other entertainment shows. The tiger featured prominently in the ancient [mythology](/wiki/Mythology "Mythology") and [folklore](/wiki/Folklore "Folklore") of cultures throughout its historic range and has continued to [appear in culture](/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_tigers "Cultural depictions of tigers") worldwide.

Etymology
---------

The [Old English](/wiki/Old_English "Old English") *tigras* derives from [Old French](/wiki/Old_French "Old French") *tigre*, from [Latin](/wiki/Latin "Latin") *tigris*, which was a borrowing from *tigris* ([Ancient Greek](/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language "Ancient Greek language"): Ï„Î¯Î³ÏÎ¹Ï‚).[[4]](#cite_note-4)
Since ancient times, the word *tigris* has been suggested to originate from the [Armenian](/wiki/Armenian_language "Armenian language") or [Persian](/wiki/Persian_language "Persian language") word for 'arrow', which may also be the origin of the name for the river [Tigris](/wiki/Tigris "Tigris").[[5]](#cite_note-Varro-5)[[6]](#cite_note-Thorley-6) However, today, the names are thought to be [homonyms](/wiki/Homonyms "Homonyms"), and the connection between the tiger and the river is doubted.[[6]](#cite_note-Thorley-6)

Taxonomy
--------

In 1758, [Carl Linnaeus](/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus "Carl Linnaeus") described the tiger in his work *[Systema Naturae](/wiki/Systema_Naturae "Systema Naturae")* and gave it the [scientific name](/wiki/Scientific_name "Scientific name") *Felis tigris*, as the genus *Felis* was being used for all cats at the time. His [scientific description](/wiki/Scientific_description "Scientific description") was based on descriptions by earlier naturalists such as [Conrad Gessner](/wiki/Conrad_Gessner "Conrad Gessner") and [Ulisse Aldrovandi](/wiki/Ulisse_Aldrovandi "Ulisse Aldrovandi").[[2]](#cite_note-Linn1758-2) In 1929, [Reginald Innes Pocock](/wiki/Reginald_Innes_Pocock "Reginald Innes Pocock") placed the species in the genus *[Panthera](/wiki/Panthera "Panthera")* using the scientific name *Panthera tigris*.[[7]](#cite_note-pocock1929-7)[[8]](#cite_note-pocock1939-8)

### Subspecies

Nine [recent](/wiki/Holocene "Holocene") tiger [subspecies](/wiki/Subspecies "Subspecies") have been proposed between the early 19th and early 21st centuries, namely the [Bengal](/wiki/Bengal_tiger "Bengal tiger"), [Malayan](/wiki/Malayan_tiger "Malayan tiger"), [Indochinese](/wiki/Indochinese_tiger "Indochinese tiger"), [South China](/wiki/South_China_tiger "South China tiger"), [Siberian](/wiki/Siberian_tiger "Siberian tiger"), [Caspian](/wiki/Caspian_tiger "Caspian tiger"), [Javan](/wiki/Javan_tiger "Javan tiger"), [Bali](/wiki/Bali_tiger "Bali tiger") and [Sumatran tigers](/wiki/Sumatran_tiger "Sumatran tiger").[[9]](#cite_note-MSW3-9)[[10]](#cite_note-Wilting2015-10) The [validity](/wiki/Valid_name_(zoology) "Valid name (zoology)") of several tiger subspecies was questioned in 1999 as most putative subspecies were distinguished on the basis of fur length and colouration, striping patterns and body size of specimens in [natural history museum](/wiki/Natural_history_museum "Natural history museum") collections that are not necessarily representative for the entire population. It was proposed to recognise only two tiger subspecies as valid, namely *P. t. tigris* in mainland Asia and the smaller *P. t. sondaica* in the [Greater Sunda Islands](/wiki/Greater_Sunda_Islands "Greater Sunda Islands").[[11]](#cite_note-Kitchener1999-11)

This two-subspecies proposal was reaffirmed in 2015 through a comprehensive analysis of morphological, ecological and [mitochondrial DNA](/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA "Mitochondrial DNA") (mtDNA) traits of all putative tiger subspecies.[[10]](#cite_note-Wilting2015-10)
In 2017, the Cat Classification Task Force of the IUCN Cat Specialist Group revised [felid](/wiki/Felidae "Felidae") taxonomy in accordance with the 2015 two-subspecies proposal and recognised only *P. t. tigris* and *P. t. sondaica*.[[12]](#cite_note-catsg-12) Results of a 2018 [whole-genome sequencing](/wiki/Whole-genome_sequencing "Whole-genome sequencing") study of 32 samples from the six living putative subspeciesâ€”the Bengal, Malayan, Indochinese, South China, Siberian and Sumatran tigerâ€”found them to be distinct and separate [clades](/wiki/Clade "Clade").[[13]](#cite_note-13) These results were corroborated in 2021 and 2023.[[14]](#cite_note-14)[[15]](#cite_note-Wang2023-15) A 2023 study found validity for all nine recent subspecies.[[16]](#cite_note-Sun2023-16) The Cat Specialist Group states that "Given the varied interpretations of data, the [subspecific] taxonomy of this species is currently under review by the IUCN SSC Cat Specialist Group."[[17]](#cite_note-17)

The following tables are based on the [classification](/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology) "Taxonomy (biology)") of the tiger as of 2005,[[9]](#cite_note-MSW3-9) and also reflect the classification recognised by the Cat Classification Task Force in 2017.[[12]](#cite_note-catsg-12)

*Panthera tigris tigris* (Linnaeus, 1758)[[2]](#cite_note-Linn1758-2)

| Population | Description | Image |
| --- | --- | --- |
| [Bengal tiger](/wiki/Bengal_tiger "Bengal tiger") formerly *P. t. tigris* (Linnaeus, 1758)[[2]](#cite_note-Linn1758-2) | This population inhabits the [Indian subcontinent](/wiki/Indian_subcontinent "Indian subcontinent").[[18]](#cite_note-Jackson1996-18) The Bengal tiger has shorter fur than tigers further north,[[8]](#cite_note-pocock1939-8) with a light [tawny](/wiki/Tawny_(color) "Tawny (color)") to orange-red colouration,[[8]](#cite_note-pocock1939-8)[[19]](#cite_note-19) and relatively long and narrow nostrils.[[20]](#cite_note-Maz%C3%A1k2010-20) |  |
| [â€ ](/wiki/Extinction "Extinction") [Caspian tiger](/wiki/Caspian_tiger "Caspian tiger") formerly *P. t. virgata* ([Illiger](/wiki/Johann_Karl_Wilhelm_Illiger "Johann Karl Wilhelm Illiger"), 1815)[[21]](#cite_note-Illiger-21) | This population occurred from [Turkey](/wiki/Turkey "Turkey") to around the [Caspian Sea](/wiki/Caspian_Sea "Caspian Sea").[[18]](#cite_note-Jackson1996-18) It had bright rusty-red fur with thin and closely spaced brownish stripes,[[22]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESludskii1992137-22) and a broad [occipital bone](/wiki/Occipital_bone "Occipital bone").[[11]](#cite_note-Kitchener1999-11) Genetic analysis revealed that it was closely related to the Siberian tiger.[[23]](#cite_note-Driscoll2009-23) It has been extinct since the 1970s.[[24]](#cite_note-Seidensticker1999-24) |  |
| [Siberian tiger](/wiki/Siberian_tiger "Siberian tiger") formerly *P. t. altaica* ([Temminck](/wiki/Coenraad_Jacob_Temminck "Coenraad Jacob Temminck"), 1844)[[25]](#cite_note-Temminck-25) | This population lives in the [Russian Far East](/wiki/Russian_Far_East "Russian Far East"), [Northeast China](/wiki/Northeast_China "Northeast China") and possibly [North Korea](/wiki/North_Korea "North Korea").[[18]](#cite_note-Jackson1996-18) The Siberian tiger has long hair and dense fur.[[25]](#cite_note-Temminck-25) Its ground colour varies widely from [ochre](/wiki/Ochre "Ochre")-yellow in winter to more reddish and vibrant after moulting.[[26]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESludskii1992131-26) The skull is shorter and broader than the skulls of tigers further south.[[20]](#cite_note-Maz%C3%A1k2010-20) |  |
| [South China tiger](/wiki/South_China_tiger "South China tiger") formerly *P. t. amoyensis* ([Hilzheimer](/wiki/Max_Hilzheimer "Max Hilzheimer"), 1905)[[27]](#cite_note-Hilzheimer-27) | This tiger historically lived in south-central China.[[18]](#cite_note-Jackson1996-18) The skulls of the five [type specimens](/wiki/Type_specimen "Type specimen") had shorter [carnassials](/wiki/Carnassial "Carnassial") and [molars](/wiki/Molar_(tooth) "Molar (tooth)") than tigers from India, a smaller cranium, [orbits](/wiki/Orbit_(anatomy) "Orbit (anatomy)") set closer together and larger [postorbital processes](/wiki/Orbital_process_of_the_zygomatic_bone "Orbital process of the zygomatic bone"); skins were yellowish with [rhombus](/wiki/Rhombus "Rhombus")-like stripes.[[27]](#cite_note-Hilzheimer-27) It has a unique mtDNA [haplotype](/wiki/Haplotype "Haplotype") due to interbreeding with ancient tiger lineages.[[12]](#cite_note-catsg-12)[[16]](#cite_note-Sun2023-16)[[28]](#cite_note-Hu2022-28) It is [extinct in the wild](/wiki/Extinct_in_the_wild "Extinct in the wild") as there has not been a confirmed sighting since the 1970s,[[1]](#cite_note-iucn-1) and survives only in captivity.[[15]](#cite_note-Wang2023-15) |  |
| [Indochinese tiger](/wiki/Indochinese_tiger "Indochinese tiger") formerly *P. t. corbetti* ([MazÃ¡k](/wiki/Vratislav_Maz%C3%A1k "Vratislav MazÃ¡k"), 1968)[[29]](#cite_note-Mazak1968-29) | This tiger population occurs on the [Indochinese Peninsula](/wiki/Indochinese_Peninsula "Indochinese Peninsula").[[18]](#cite_note-Jackson1996-18) Indochinese tiger specimens have smaller craniums than Bengal tigers and appear to have darker fur with somewhat thin stripes.[[29]](#cite_note-Mazak1968-29)[[30]](#cite_note-mazak06-30) |  |
| [Malayan tiger](/wiki/Malayan_tiger "Malayan tiger") formerly *P. t. jacksoni* (Luo et al., 2004)[[31]](#cite_note-Luo04-31) | The Malayan tiger was proposed as a distinct subspecies on the basis of mtDNA and [micro-satellite sequences](/wiki/DNA_sequences "DNA sequences") that differ from the Indochinese tiger.[[31]](#cite_note-Luo04-31) It does not differ significantly in fur colour or skull size from Indochinese tigers.[[30]](#cite_note-mazak06-30) There is no clear geographical barrier between tiger populations in northern [Malaysia](/wiki/Malaysia "Malaysia") and southern [Thailand](/wiki/Thailand "Thailand").[[1]](#cite_note-iucn-1) |  |

*Panthera tigris sondaica* (Temminck, 1844)[[12]](#cite_note-catsg-12)

| â€ [Javan tiger](/wiki/Javan_tiger "Javan tiger") formerly *P. t. sondaica* (Temminck, 1944)[[25]](#cite_note-Temminck-25) | This tiger was described based on an unspecified number of skins with short and smooth hair.[[25]](#cite_note-Temminck-25) Tigers from [Java](/wiki/Java "Java") were small compared to tigers of the Asian mainland, had relatively elongated skulls compared to the Sumatran tiger and longer, thinner and more numerous stripes.[[30]](#cite_note-mazak06-30) The Javan tiger is thought to have gone extinct by the 1980s.[[24]](#cite_note-Seidensticker1999-24) |  |
