| [Sumatran tiger](/wiki/Sumatran_tiger "Sumatran tiger") formerly *P. t. sumatrae* ([Pocock](/wiki/Reginald_Innes_Pocock "Reginald Innes Pocock"), 1929)[[35]](#cite_note-Pocock1929-35) | The type specimen from [Sumatra](/wiki/Sumatra "Sumatra") had dark fur.[[35]](#cite_note-Pocock1929-35) The Sumatran tiger has particularly long hair around the face,[[18]](#cite_note-Jackson1996-18) thick body stripes and a broader and smaller [nasal bone](/wiki/Nasal_bone "Nasal bone") than other island tigers.[[30]](#cite_note-mazak06-30) |  |

### Evolution

|  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  | | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | | *[Panthera](/wiki/Panthera "Panthera")* | |  |  |  |  |  |  |  |  | | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | --- | |  | |  |  | | --- | --- | |  | [Snow leopard](/wiki/Snow_leopard "Snow leopard") | |  | |  | **Tiger** | |  | | |  | |  | |  |  | | --- | --- | |  | [Jaguar](/wiki/Jaguar "Jaguar") | |  | |  | |  |  | | --- | --- | |  | [Leopard](/wiki/Leopard "Leopard") | |  | |  | [Lion](/wiki/Lion "Lion") | |  | | |  | | |  | | |  | |
| Phylogeny of the genus *Panthera* based on a 2016 [nuclear DNA](/wiki/Nuclear_DNA "Nuclear DNA") study[[36]](#cite_note-Li_al2016-36) |

The tiger shares the genus *Panthera* with the [lion](/wiki/Lion "Lion"), [leopard](/wiki/Leopard "Leopard"), [jaguar](/wiki/Jaguar "Jaguar") and [snow leopard](/wiki/Snow_leopard "Snow leopard"). Results of genetic analyses indicate that the tiger and snow leopard are [sister species](/wiki/Sister_taxon "Sister taxon") whose [lineages](/wiki/Lineage_(evolution) "Lineage (evolution)") split from each other between 2.70 and 3.70 million years ago.[[37]](#cite_note-37) The tiger's whole genome sequencing shows [repeated sequences](/wiki/Repeated_sequence_(DNA) "Repeated sequence (DNA)") that parallel those in other cat genomes.[[38]](#cite_note-38)

The fossil species *[Panthera palaeosinensis](/wiki/Panthera_palaeosinensis "Panthera palaeosinensis")* of early [Pleistocene](/wiki/Pleistocene "Pleistocene") northern China was described as a possible tiger ancestor when it was discovered in 1924, but modern cladistics places it as [basal](/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics) "Basal (phylogenetics)") to modern *Panthera*.[[39]](#cite_note-Maz%C3%A1k-39)[[40]](#cite_note-Tseng-40)*[Panthera zdanskyi](/wiki/Panthera_zdanskyi "Panthera zdanskyi")* lived around the same time and place, and was suggested to be a sister species of the modern tiger when it was examined in 2014.[[39]](#cite_note-Maz%C3%A1k-39) However, as of 2023, at least two subsequent studies considered *P. zdanskyi* likely to be a [synonym](/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy) "Synonym (taxonomy)") of *P. palaeosinensis*, noting that its proposed differences from that species fell within the range of individual variation.[[41]](#cite_note-41)[[42]](#cite_note-42) The earliest appearance of the modern tiger species in the fossil record are jaw fragments from [Lantion](/wiki/Lantian_County "Lantian County") in China that are dated to the early Pleistocene.[[39]](#cite_note-Maz%C3%A1k-39)

Middle- to late-Pleistocene tiger fossils have been found throughout China, Sumatra and Java. Prehistoric subspecies include *[Panthera tigris trinilensis](/wiki/Panthera_tigris_trinilensis "Panthera tigris trinilensis")* and *[P. t. soloensis](/wiki/Panthera_tigris_soloensis "Panthera tigris soloensis")* of Java and Sumatra and *[P. t. acutidens](/wiki/Panthera_tigris_acutidens "Panthera tigris acutidens")* of China; late Pleistocene and early [Holocene](/wiki/Holocene "Holocene") fossils of tigers have also been found in [Borneo](/wiki/Bornean_tiger "Bornean tiger") and [Palawan](/wiki/Palawan "Palawan"), Philippines.[[43]](#cite_note-Kitchener2009-43) Fossil specimens of tigers have also been reported from the Middle-Late Pleistocene of Japan.[[44]](#cite_note-44) Results of a [phylogeographic](/wiki/Phylogeographic "Phylogeographic") study indicate that all living tigers have a common ancestor that lived between 108,000 and 72,000 years ago.[[31]](#cite_note-Luo04-31) Genetic studies suggest that the tiger population contracted around 115,000 years ago due to glaciation. Modern tiger populations originated from a [refugium](/wiki/Refugium_(population_biology) "Refugium (population biology)") in Indochina and spread across Asia after the [Last Glacial Maximum](/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum "Last Glacial Maximum"). As they colonised northeastern China, the ancestors of the South China tiger intermixed with a relict tiger population.[[28]](#cite_note-Hu2022-28)[[16]](#cite_note-Sun2023-16)

### Hybrids

Further information: [Felid hybrids](/wiki/Felid_hybrids "Felid hybrids") and [Panthera hybrid](/wiki/Panthera_hybrid "Panthera hybrid")

Tigers can [interbreed](/wiki/Interbreed "Interbreed") with other *Panthera* cats and have done so in captivity. The [liger](/wiki/Liger "Liger") is the offspring of a female tiger and a male lion and the [tigon](/wiki/Tigon "Tigon") the offspring of a male tiger and a female lion.[[45]](#cite_note-Gabry%C5%9B-45) The lion sire passes on a growth-promoting gene, but the corresponding growth-inhibiting gene from the female tiger is absent, so that ligers grow far larger than either parent species. By contrast, the male tiger does not pass on a growth-promoting gene while the lioness passes on a growth inhibiting gene; hence, tigons are around the same size as their parents.[[46]](#cite_note-imprinting-46) Since they often develop life-threatening birth defects and can easily become obese, breeding these hybrids is regarded as unethical.[[45]](#cite_note-Gabry%C5%9B-45)

Description
-----------

[![Drawing of tiger skeleton](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/TigerSkelLyd1.png/250px-TigerSkelLyd1.png)](/wiki/File:TigerSkelLyd1.png)

Tiger skeleton from Royal Natural History Volume 1 (1839)

The tiger has a typical felid morphology, with a muscular body, shortened legs, strong forelimbs with wide front paws, a large head and a tail that is about half the length of the rest of its body.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[48]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESludskii199298-48) It has five digits, including a [dewclaw](/wiki/Dewclaw "Dewclaw"), on the front feet and four on the back, all of which have retractile claws that are compact and curved, and can reach 10Â cm (3.9Â in) long.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[49]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200426-49) The ears are rounded and the eyes have a round pupil.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) The snout ends in a triangular, pink tip with small black dots, the number of which increase with age.[[50]](#cite_note-50) The tiger's skull is robust, with a constricted front region, proportionally small, elliptical [orbits](/wiki/Orbit_(anatomy) "Orbit (anatomy)"), long [nasal bones](/wiki/Nasal_bone "Nasal bone") and a lengthened [cranium](/wiki/Cranium "Cranium") with a large [sagittal crest](/wiki/Sagittal_crest "Sagittal crest").[[51]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESludskii1992103-51)[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) It resembles a lion's skull, but differs from it in the concave or flattened underside of the lower jaw and in its longer nasals.[[51]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESludskii1992103-51)[[43]](#cite_note-Kitchener2009-43) The tiger has 30 fairly robust teeth and its somewhat curved [canines](/wiki/Canine_tooth "Canine tooth") are the longest in the cat family at 6.4â€“7.6Â cm (2.5â€“3.0Â in).[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[52]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200425-52)

The tiger has a head-body length of 1.4â€“2.8Â m (4Â ft 7Â inÂ â€“ 9Â ft 2Â in) with a 0.6â€“1.1Â m (2Â ft 0Â inÂ â€“ 3Â ft 7Â in) tail and stands 0.8â€“1.1Â m (2Â ft 7Â inÂ â€“ 3Â ft 7Â in) at the shoulder.[[53]](#cite_note-Walker-53) The Siberian and Bengal tigers are the largest.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) Male Bengal tigers weigh 200â€“260Â kg (440â€“570Â lb), and females weigh 100â€“160Â kg (220â€“350Â lb); island tigers are the smallest, likely due to [insular dwarfism](/wiki/Insular_dwarfism "Insular dwarfism").[[11]](#cite_note-Kitchener1999-11) Male Sumatran tigers weigh 100â€“140Â kg (220â€“310Â lb), and females weigh 75â€“110Â kg (165â€“243Â lb).[[54]](#cite_note-Sunquist2010-54) The tiger is popularly thought to be the largest living felid species; but since tigers of the different subspecies and populations vary greatly in size and weight, the tiger's average size may be less than the lion's, while the largest tigers are bigger than their lion counterparts.[[43]](#cite_note-Kitchener2009-43)

### Coat

[![Close up of a tiger's fur](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Tiger_Stripes_%2829808869755%29.jpg/250px-Tiger_Stripes_%2829808869755%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tiger_Stripes_(29808869755).jpg)

Siberian tiger coat on flank (side)

The tiger's coat usually has short hairs, reaching up to 35Â mm (1.4Â in), though the hairs of the northern-living Siberian tiger can reach 105Â mm (4.1Â in). Belly hairs tend to be longer than back hairs. The density of their fur is usually thin, though the Siberian tiger develops a particularly thick winter coat. The tiger has lines of fur around the face and long whiskers, especially in males.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) It has an orange [colouration](/wiki/Animal_coloration "Animal coloration") that varies from yellowish to reddish.[[55]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200428-55) White fur covers the underside, from head to tail, along with the inner surface of the legs and parts of the face.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[56]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESludskii199299%E2%80%93102-56) On the back of the ears, it has a prominent white spot, which is surrounded by black.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) The tiger is marked with distinctive black or dark brown stripes, which are uniquely patterned in each individual.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[57]](#cite_note-Miquelle-57) The stripes are mostly vertical, but those on the limbs and forehead are horizontal. They are more concentrated towards the backside and those on the trunk may reach under the belly. The tips of stripes are generally sharp and some may split up or split and fuse again. Tail stripes are thick bands and a black tip marks the end.[[56]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESludskii199299%E2%80%93102-56)

The tiger is one of only a few striped cat species.[[58]](#cite_note-58) Stripes are advantageous for [camouflage](/wiki/Camouflage "Camouflage") in vegetation with vertical patterns of light and shade, such as trees, reeds and tall grass.[[57]](#cite_note-Miquelle-57)[[59]](#cite_note-59) This is supported by a [Fourier analysis](/wiki/Fourier_analysis "Fourier analysis") study showing that the striping patterns line up with their environment.[[60]](#cite_note-60)
The orange colour may also aid in concealment, as the tiger's prey is [colour blind](/wiki/Color_blindness "Color blindness") and possibly perceives the tiger as green and blended in with the vegetation.[[61]](#cite_note-61)

#### Colour variations

[![White tiger with thickened stripes lying down](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/White_tiger_Nandankanan.jpeg/250px-White_tiger_Nandankanan.jpeg)](/wiki/File:White_tiger_Nandankanan.jpeg)

Pseudo-melanistic [white tiger](/wiki/White_tiger "White tiger")

The three [colour variants](/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology) "Polymorphism (biology)") of Bengal tigers â€“ nearly stripeless snow-white, white and golden â€“ are now virtually non-existent in the wild due to the reduction of wild tiger populations but continue in captive populations. The [white tiger](/wiki/White_tiger "White tiger") has a white background colour with [sepia](/wiki/Sepia_(color) "Sepia (color)")-brown stripes. The [golden tiger](/wiki/Golden_tiger "Golden tiger") is pale golden with reddish-brown stripes. The snow-white tiger is a morph with extremely faint stripes and a pale sepia-brown ringed tail. White and golden morphs are the result of an [autosomal recessive trait](/wiki/Autosomal_recessive_trait "Autosomal recessive trait") with a white [locus](/wiki/Locus_(genetics) "Locus (genetics)") and a [wideband](/wiki/Cat_coat_genetics#Wide_band_factors "Cat coat genetics") locus, respectively. The snow-white variation is caused by [polygenes](/wiki/Polygene "Polygene") with both white and wideband loci.[[62]](#cite_note-Xu_al2017-62) The breeding of white tigers is controversial, as they have no use for conservation. Only 0.001% of wild tigers have the genes for this colour morph and the overrepresentation of white tigers in captivity is the result of [inbreeding](/wiki/Inbreeding "Inbreeding"). Hence, their continued breeding will risk both [inbreeding depression](/wiki/Inbreeding_depression "Inbreeding depression") and loss of [genetic variability](/wiki/Genetic_variability "Genetic variability") in captive tigers.[[63]](#cite_note-63)

Pseudo-[melanistic](/wiki/Melanistic "Melanistic") tigers with thick, merged stripes have been recorded in [Simlipal National Park](/wiki/Simlipal_National_Park "Simlipal National Park") and three Indian zoos; a [population genetic](/wiki/Population_genetics "Population genetics") analysis of Indian tiger samples revealed that this [phenotype](/wiki/Phenotype "Phenotype") is caused by a [mutation](/wiki/Mutation "Mutation") of a [transmembrane](/wiki/Transmembrane_protein "Transmembrane protein") [aminopeptidase](/wiki/Aminopeptidase "Aminopeptidase") gene. Around 37% of the Simlipal tiger population has this feature, which has been linked to [genetic isolation](/wiki/Genetic_isolate "Genetic isolate").[[64]](#cite_note-64)

Distribution and habitat
------------------------

[![Picture of tiger in forest at night](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Tigress_Anna_Savelevna_01.jpg/250px-Tigress_Anna_Savelevna_01.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tigress_Anna_Savelevna_01.jpg)

Camera trap of a [Siberian tiger](/wiki/Siberian_tiger "Siberian tiger") in Russia

The tiger historically ranged from eastern Turkey, northern Iran and Afghanistan to Central Asia and from northern Pakistan through the [Indian subcontinent](/wiki/Indian_subcontinent "Indian subcontinent") and Indochina to southeastern Siberia, Sumatra, Java and Bali.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) As of 2022, it inhabits less than 7% of its historical distribution and has a scattered range in the Indian subcontinent, the [Indochinese Peninsula](/wiki/Indochinese_Peninsula "Indochinese Peninsula"), Sumatra, northeastern China and the [Russian Far East](/wiki/Russian_Far_East "Russian Far East").[[1]](#cite_note-iucn-1) As of 2020, India had the largest extent of global tiger habitat with 300,508Â km2 (116,027Â sqÂ mi), followed by Russia with 195,819Â km2 (75,606Â sqÂ mi).[[65]](#cite_note-Sanderson_al2023-65)

The tiger mainly lives in forest habitats and is highly adaptable.[[54]](#cite_note-Sunquist2010-54) Records in Central Asia indicate that it primarily inhabited [Tugay](/wiki/Tugay "Tugay") riverine forests and hilly and lowland forests in the [Caucasus](/wiki/Caucasus "Caucasus").[[66]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESludskii1992108%E2%80%93112-66) In the [Amur](/wiki/Amur_River "Amur River")-[Ussuri](/wiki/Ussuri_River "Ussuri River") region of Russia and China, it inhabits [Korean pine](/wiki/Korean_pine "Korean pine") and [temperate broadleaf and mixed forests](/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forest "Temperate broadleaf and mixed forest"); [riparian forests](/wiki/Riparian_forest "Riparian forest") serve as [dispersal](/wiki/Biological_dispersal "Biological dispersal") corridors, providing food and water for both tigers and [ungulates](/wiki/Ungulate "Ungulate").[[67]](#cite_note-Miquelle_al1999-67) On the Indian subcontinent, it inhabits mainly [tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests](/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests "Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests"), [temperate broadleaf and mixed forests](/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forests "Temperate broadleaf and mixed forests"), [tropical moist evergreen forests](/wiki/Tropical_evergreen_forests_of_India "Tropical evergreen forests of India"), [tropical dry forests](/wiki/Tropical_dry_forest "Tropical dry forest"), [alluvial plains](/wiki/Alluvial_plain "Alluvial plain") and the [mangrove forests](/wiki/Mangrove_forest "Mangrove forest") of the [Sundarbans](/wiki/Sundarbans "Sundarbans").[[68]](#cite_note-Wikramanayake_al1999-68) In the [Eastern Himalayas](/wiki/Eastern_Himalaya "Eastern Himalaya"), it was documented in [temperate forest](/wiki/Temperate_forest "Temperate forest") up to an elevation of 4,200Â m (13,800Â ft) in Bhutan, of 3,630Â m (11,910Â ft) in the [Mishmi Hills](/wiki/Mishmi_Hills "Mishmi Hills") and of 3,139Â m (10,299Â ft) in [MÃªdog County](/wiki/M%C3%AAdog_County "MÃªdog County"), southeastern Tibet.[[69]](#cite_note-69)[[70]](#cite_note-70)[[71]](#cite_note-71) In Thailand, it lives in [deciduous](/wiki/Deciduous "Deciduous") and [evergreen](/wiki/Evergreen "Evergreen") forests.[[72]](#cite_note-72) In Sumatra, it inhabits lowland [peat swamp forests](/wiki/Peat_swamp_forest "Peat swamp forest") and rugged [montane forests](/wiki/Montane_forest "Montane forest").[[73]](#cite_note-73)

### Population density

[Camera trapping](/wiki/Camera_trap "Camera trap") during 2010â€“2015 in the deciduous and subtropical pine forest of [Jim Corbett National Park](/wiki/Jim_Corbett_National_Park "Jim Corbett National Park"), northern India revealed a stable tiger [population density](/wiki/Population_density "Population density") of 12â€“17 individuals per 100Â km2 (39Â sqÂ mi) in an area of 521Â km2 (201Â sqÂ mi).[[74]](#cite_note-74)
In northern Myanmar, the population density in a sampled area of roughly 3,250Â km2 (1,250Â sqÂ mi) in a mosaic of tropical broadleaf forest and grassland was estimated to be 0.21â€“0.44 tigers per 100Â km2 (39Â sqÂ mi) as of 2009.[[75]](#cite_note-75)
Population density in mixed deciduous and semi-evergreen forests of Thailand's [Huai Kha Khaeng Wildlife Sanctuary](/wiki/Huai_Kha_Khaeng_Wildlife_Sanctuary "Huai Kha Khaeng Wildlife Sanctuary") was estimated at 2.01 tigers per 100Â km2 (39Â sqÂ mi); during the 1970s and 1980s, [logging](/wiki/Logging "Logging") and poaching had occurred in the adjacent [Mae Wong](/wiki/Mae_Wong_National_Park "Mae Wong National Park") and [Khlong Lan National Parks](/wiki/Khlong_Lan_National_Park "Khlong Lan National Park"), where population density was much lower, estimated at only 0.359 tigers per 100Â km2 (39Â sqÂ mi) as of 2016.[[76]](#cite_note-76)
Population density in [dipterocarp](/wiki/Dipterocarpaceae "Dipterocarpaceae") and montane forests in northern Malaysia was estimated at 1.47â€“2.43 adult tigers per 100Â km2 (39Â sqÂ mi) in [Royal Belum State Park](/wiki/Royal_Belum_State_Park "Royal Belum State Park"), but 0.3â€“0.92 adult tigers per 100Â km2 (39Â sqÂ mi) in the unprotected [selectively logged](/wiki/Selective_logging "Selective logging") Temengor Forest Reserve.[[77]](#cite_note-77)

Behaviour and ecology
---------------------

[![Tiger in water](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Tigerwater_edit2.jpg/250px-Tigerwater_edit2.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tigerwater_edit2.jpg)

Tiger bathing

Camera trap data show that tigers in [Chitwan National Park](/wiki/Chitwan_National_Park "Chitwan National Park") avoided locations frequented by people and were more active at night than during day.[[78]](#cite_note-78)
In [Sundarbans National Park](/wiki/Sundarbans_National_Park "Sundarbans National Park"), six [radio-collared](/wiki/Radio_collar "Radio collar") tigers were most active from dawn to early morning and reached their zenith around 7:00 o'clock in the morning.[[79]](#cite_note-79)
A three-year-long camera trap survey in [Shuklaphanta National Park](/wiki/Shuklaphanta_National_Park "Shuklaphanta National Park") revealed that tigers were most active from dusk until midnight.[[80]](#cite_note-80)
In northeastern China, tigers were [crepuscular](/wiki/Crepuscular_animal "Crepuscular animal") and active at night with activity peaking at dawn and dusk; they were largely active at the same time as their prey.[[81]](#cite_note-81)

The tiger is a powerful swimmer and easily transverses rivers as wide as 8Â km (5.0Â mi); it immerses in water, particularly on hot days.[[57]](#cite_note-Miquelle-57) In general, it is less capable of climbing trees than many other cats due to its size, but cubs under 16 months old may routinely do so.[[82]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200426,_64%E2%80%9366-82) An adult was recorded climbing 10Â m (33Â ft) up a smooth [pipal tree](/wiki/Ficus_religiosa "Ficus religiosa").[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)

### Social spacing

Adult tigers lead largely solitary lives within [home ranges](/wiki/Home_range "Home range") or [territories](/wiki/Territory_(animal) "Territory (animal)"), the size of which mainly depends on prey abundance, geographic area and sex of the individual. Males and females defend their home ranges from those of the same sex and the home range of a male encompasses that of multiple females.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[57]](#cite_note-Miquelle-57) Two females in the [Sundarbans](/wiki/Sundarbans "Sundarbans") had home ranges of 10.6 and 14.1Â km2 (4.1 and 5.4Â sqÂ mi).[[83]](#cite_note-83)
In [Panna Tiger Reserve](/wiki/Panna_Tiger_Reserve "Panna Tiger Reserve"), the home ranges of five reintroduced females varied from 53â€“67Â km2 (20â€“26Â sqÂ mi) in winter to 55â€“60Â km2 (21â€“23Â sqÂ mi) in summer and to 46â€“94Â km2 (18â€“36Â sqÂ mi) during the [monsoon](/wiki/Monsoon "Monsoon"); three males had 84â€“147Â km2 (32â€“57Â sqÂ mi) large home ranges in winter, 82â€“98Â km2 (32â€“38Â sqÂ mi) in summer and 81â€“118Â km2 (31â€“46Â sqÂ mi) during monsoon seasons.[[84]](#cite_note-Sarkar2016-84)
In [Sikhote-Alin Biosphere Reserve](/wiki/Sikhote-Alin_Biosphere_Reserve "Sikhote-Alin Biosphere Reserve"), 14 females had home ranges 248â€“520Â km2 (96â€“201Â sqÂ mi) and five resident males of 847â€“1,923Â km2 (327â€“742Â sqÂ mi) that overlapped with those of up to five females.[[85]](#cite_note-Goodrich_2010-85) When tigresses in the same reserve had cubs of up to four months of age, they reduced their home ranges to stay near their young and steadily enlarged them until their offspring were 13â€“18 months old.[[86]](#cite_note-86)

[![Tiger spray marking. DavidRaju 1.jpg](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Tiger_spray_marking._DavidRaju_1.jpg/330px-Tiger_spray_marking._DavidRaju_1.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tiger_spray_marking._DavidRaju_1.jpg)

[![Bijili (A tiger in Ranthambore National Park, 2016) 1.jpg](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Bijili_%28A_tiger_in_Ranthambore_National_Park%2C_2016%29_1.jpg/250px-Bijili_%28A_tiger_in_Ranthambore_National_Park%2C_2016%29_1.jpg)](/wiki/File:Bijili_(A_tiger_in_Ranthambore_National_Park,_2016)_1.jpg)

[Bengal tigers](/wiki/Bengal_tiger "Bengal tiger") spraying urine (above) and rubbing against a tree to mark territory

The tiger is a long-ranging species and individuals disperse over distances of up to 650Â km (400Â mi) to reach tiger populations in other areas.[[87]](#cite_note-87) Young tigresses establish their first home ranges close to their mothers' while males migrate further than their female counterparts.[[88]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200476-88) Four [radio-collared](/wiki/Radio_telemetry "Radio telemetry") females in Chitwan dispersed between 0 and 43.2Â km (0.0 and 26.8Â mi) and 10 males between 9.5 and 65.7Â km (5.9 and 40.8Â mi).[[89]](#cite_note-Smith1993-89) A subadult male lives as a transient in another male's home range until he is older and strong enough to challenge the resident male.[[88]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200476-88)[[90]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200454%E2%80%9355-90) Tigers mark their home ranges by [spraying urine](/wiki/Urine_spraying "Urine spraying") on vegetation and rocks, clawing or [scent rubbing](/wiki/Scent_rubbing "Scent rubbing") trees and marking trails with [faeces](/wiki/Faeces "Faeces"), [anal gland](/wiki/Anal_gland "Anal gland") secretions and ground scrapings.[[57]](#cite_note-Miquelle-57)[[91]](#cite_note-91)[[92]](#cite_note-92)[[93]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004105-93) Scent markings also allow an individual to pick up information on another's identity. Unclaimed home ranges, particularly those that belonged to a deceased individual, can be taken over in days or weeks.[[57]](#cite_note-Miquelle-57)

Male tigers are generally less tolerant of other males within their home ranges than females are of other females. Disputes are usually solved by intimidation rather than fighting. Once [dominance](/wiki/Dominance_(ethology) "Dominance (ethology)") has been established, a male may tolerate a subordinate within his range, as long as they do not come near him. The most serious disputes tend to occur between two males competing for a female in [oestrus](/wiki/Oestrus "Oestrus").[[94]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200485%E2%80%9386-94) Though tigers mostly live alone, relationships between individuals can be complex. Tigers are particularly social at kills and a male tiger will sometimes share a carcass with the females and cubs within this home range and unlike male lions, will allow them to feed on the kill before he is finished with it. However, a female is more tense when encountering another female at a kill.[[95]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967244%E2%80%93251-95)[[96]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200489-96)

### Communication

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Panthera_tigris_altaica_28_-_Buffalo_Zoo_%281%29.jpg/250px-Panthera_tigris_altaica_28_-_Buffalo_Zoo_%281%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Panthera_tigris_altaica_28_-_Buffalo_Zoo_(1).jpg)

Siberian tiger baring teeth as a sign of aggression

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Sumatran_tiger_%28Panthera_tigris_sumatrae%29_vocalising.webm/138px--Sumatran_tiger_%28Panthera_tigris_sumatrae%29_vocalising.webm.jpg)](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/42/Sumatran_tiger_%28Panthera_tigris_sumatrae%29_vocalising.webm/Sumatran_tiger_%28Panthera_tigris_sumatrae%29_vocalising.webm.480p.vp9.webm)

Captive [Sumatran tiger](/wiki/Sumatran_tiger "Sumatran tiger") roaring

During friendly encounters and bonding, tigers [rub against](/wiki/Bunting_(animal_behavior) "Bunting (animal behavior)") each other's bodies.[[97]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967262%E2%80%93263-97) Facial expressions include the "defence threat", which involves a wrinkled face, bared teeth, pulled-back ears and widened pupils.[[98]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967263-98)[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) Both males and females show a [flehmen response](/wiki/Flehmen_response "Flehmen response"), a characteristic curled-lip grimace, when smelling urine markings. Males also use the flehmen to detect the markings made by tigresses in oestrus.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) Tigers will move their ears around to display the white spots, particularly during aggressive encounters and between mothers and cubs.[[99]](#cite_note-WCW-99) They also use their tails to signal their mood. To show cordiality, the tail sticks up and sways slowly, while an apprehensive tiger lowers its tail or wags it side-to-side. When calm, the tail hangs low.[[100]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200429-100)

Tigers are normally silent but can produce numerous vocalisations.[[101]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967256-101)[[102]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200499-102) They [roar](/wiki/Roar "Roar") to signal their presence to other individuals over long distances. This vocalisation is forced through an open mouth as it closes and can be heard 3Â km (1.9Â mi) away. They roar multiple times in a row and others respond in kind. Tigers also roar during mating and a mother will roar to call her cubs to her. When tense, tigers moan, a sound similar to a roar but softer and made when the mouth is at least partially closed. Moaning can be heard 400Â m (1,300Â ft) away.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[103]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967258%E2%80%93261-103) Aggressive encounters involve [growling](/wiki/Growling "Growling"), [snarling](/wiki/Snarling "Snarling") and hissing.[[104]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967261-104) An explosive "coughing roar" or "coughing snarl" is emitted through an open mouth and exposed teeth.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[104]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967261-104)[[99]](#cite_note-WCW-99) In friendlier situations, tigers [prusten](/wiki/Prusten "Prusten"), a soft, low-frequency snorting sound similar to [purring](/wiki/Purring "Purring") in smaller cats.[[105]](#cite_note-105) Tiger mothers communicate with their cubs by grunting, while cubs call back with [miaows](/wiki/Miaow "Miaow").[[106]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967257%E2%80%93258-106) When startled, they "woof". They produce a deer-like "pok" sound for unknown reasons, but most often at kills.[[107]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967256%E2%80%93258-107)[[108]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200462-108)

### Hunting and diet

[![Tiger attacking a sambar deer from behind, pulling on its back](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/RANTHAMBORE_TIGER_RESERVE.jpg/250px-RANTHAMBORE_TIGER_RESERVE.jpg)](/wiki/File:RANTHAMBORE_TIGER_RESERVE.jpg)

Bengal tiger attacking a [sambar deer](/wiki/Sambar_deer "Sambar deer") in [Ranthambore Tiger Reserve](/wiki/Ranthambore_Tiger_Reserve "Ranthambore Tiger Reserve")

The tiger is a [carnivore](/wiki/Carnivore "Carnivore") and an [apex predator](/wiki/Apex_predator "Apex predator"). [Abundance](/wiki/Abundance_(ecology) "Abundance (ecology)") and body weight of prey species are assumed to be the main criteria for the tiger's prey selection, both inside and outside protected areas.[[109]](#cite_note-109) It feeds mainly on large and medium-sized ungulates such as [sambar deer](/wiki/Sambar_deer "Sambar deer"), [Manchurian wapiti](/wiki/Manchurian_wapiti "Manchurian wapiti"), [barasingha](/wiki/Barasingha "Barasingha"), [gaur](/wiki/Gaur "Gaur") and [wild boar](/wiki/Wild_boar "Wild boar").[[110]](#cite_note-Hayward-110)[[111]](#cite_note-Steinmetz_al2021-111)[[112]](#cite_note-112) It also preys opportunistically on smaller species like [monkeys](/wiki/Monkey "Monkey"), [peafowl](/wiki/Peafowl "Peafowl") and other ground-based birds, [porcupines](/wiki/Porcupine "Porcupine") and fish.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[57]](#cite_note-Miquelle-57) Occasional attacks on [Asian elephants](/wiki/Asian_elephant "Asian elephant") and [Indian rhinoceroses](/wiki/Indian_rhinoceros "Indian rhinoceros") have also been reported.[[113]](#cite_note-113)
More often, tigers take the more vulnerable calves.[[114]](#cite_note-114)
They sometimes prey on livestock and dogs in close proximity to settlements.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) Tigers occasionally consume vegetation, fruit and minerals for [dietary fibre](/wiki/Dietary_fibre "Dietary fibre") and supplements.[[115]](#cite_note-Perry-115)

Tigers learn to hunt from their mothers, though the ability to hunt may be partially inborn.[[116]](#cite_note-F%C3%A0bregas-116) Depending on the size of the prey, they typically kill weekly though mothers must kill more often.[[54]](#cite_note-Sunquist2010-54) Families hunt together when cubs are old enough.[[117]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200463-117) They search for prey using vision and hearing.[[118]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967284%E2%80%93285-118) A tiger will also wait at a watering hole for prey to come by, particularly during hot summer days.[[119]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967288-119)[[120]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004120-120) It is an ambush predator and when approaching potential prey, it crouches with the head lowered and hides in foliage. It switches between creeping forward and staying still. A tiger may even doze off and can stay in the same spot for as long as a day, waiting for prey and launch an attack when the prey is close enough,[[121]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004119%E2%80%93120,_122-121) usually within 30Â m (98Â ft).[[54]](#cite_note-Sunquist2010-54) If the prey spots it before then, the cat does not pursue further.[[119]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967288-119) A tiger can sprint 56Â km/h (35Â mph) and leap 10Â m (33Â ft);[[122]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967287-122)[[123]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200423-123) it is not a long-distance runner and gives up a chase if prey outpaces it over a certain distance.[[119]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967288-119)

[![Two tigers attacking a boar](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Tiger%27s_killing_wild_boar.jpg/250px-Tiger%27s_killing_wild_boar.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tiger%27s_killing_wild_boar.jpg)

Two Bengal tigers attacking a [wild boar](/wiki/Wild_boar "Wild boar") inÂ [Kanha Tiger Reserve](/wiki/Kanha_Tiger_Reserve "Kanha Tiger Reserve")

The tiger attacks from behind or at the sides and tries to knock the target off balance. It latches onto prey with its forelimbs, twisting and turning during the struggle and tries to pull it to the ground. The tiger generally applies a [bite to the throat](/wiki/Throat_clamp "Throat clamp") until its victim dies of [strangulation](/wiki/Strangulation "Strangulation").[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[[124]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004121-124)[[125]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967295-125)[[126]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200424-126) It has an average bite force at the canine tips of 1234.3 [newtons](/wiki/Newton_(unit) "Newton (unit)").[[127]](#cite_note-127) Holding onto the throat puts the cat out of reach of horns, antlers, tusks and hooves.[[124]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004121-124)[[128]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967295%E2%80%93296-128) Tigers are adaptable killers and may use other methods, including ripping the throat or breaking the neck. Large prey may be disabled by a bite to the back of the [hock](/wiki/Hock_(anatomy) "Hock (anatomy)"), severing the tendon. Swipes from the large paws are capable of stunning or breaking the skull of a [water buffalo](/wiki/Water_buffalo "Water buffalo").[[129]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004126-129) They kill small prey with a bite to the back of the neck or head.[[130]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967289-130)[[54]](#cite_note-Sunquist2010-54) Estimates of the [success rate](/wiki/Hunting_success "Hunting success") for hunting tigers range from a low of 5% to a high of 50%. They are sometimes killed or injured by large or dangerous prey like gaur, buffalo and boar.[[54]](#cite_note-Sunquist2010-54)

Tigers typically move kills to a private, usually vegetated spot no further than 183Â m (600Â ft), though they have been recorded dragging them 549Â m (1,801Â ft). They are strong enough to drag the carcass of a fully grown buffalo for some distance. They rest for a while before eating and can consume as much as 50Â kg (110Â lb) of meat in one session, but feed on a carcass for several days, leaving little for scavengers.[[131]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967297%E2%80%93300-131)

### Competitors

[![Painting of dhole pack attacking a tiger](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Tigerdholes.jpg/250px-Tigerdholes.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tigerdholes.jpg)

An 1807 illustration of [dholes](/wiki/Dholes "Dholes") attacking a tiger

In much of their range, tigers share habitat with leopards and [dholes](/wiki/Dhole "Dhole"). They typically dominate both of them, though with dholes it depends on their pack size. Interactions between the three predators involve chasing, [stealing kills](/wiki/Kleptoparasitism "Kleptoparasitism") and direct killing.[[132]](#cite_note-132) Large dhole packs may kill tigers.[[133]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004136-133) Tigers, leopards and dholes coexist by hunting different sized prey.[[134]](#cite_note-Karanth-134) In [Nagarhole National Park](/wiki/Nagarhole_National_Park "Nagarhole National Park"), the average weight for tiger kills was found to be 91.5Â kg (202Â lb), compared to 37.6Â kg (83Â lb) for leopards and 43.4Â kg (96Â lb) for dholes.[[135]](#cite_note-KaranthSunquist1995-135) In [Kui Buri National Park](/wiki/Kui_Buri_National_Park "Kui Buri National Park"), following a reduction in prey numbers, tigers continued to kill favoured prey while leopards and dholes increased their consumption of small prey.[[111]](#cite_note-Steinmetz_al2021-111)

Both leopards and dholes can live successfully in tiger habitat when there is abundant food and vegetation cover.[[134]](#cite_note-Karanth-134)[[136]](#cite_note-136) Otherwise, they appear to be less common where tigers are numerous. The recovery of the tiger population in [Rajaji National Park](/wiki/Rajaji_National_Park "Rajaji National Park") during the 2000s led to a reduction in leopard population densities.[[137]](#cite_note-137) Similarly, at two sites in central India the size of dhole packs was negatively correlated with tiger densities.[[138]](#cite_note-138) Leopard and dhole distribution in Kui Buri correlated with both prey access and tiger scarcity.[[139]](#cite_note-139) In [Jigme Dorji National Park](/wiki/Jigme_Dorji_National_Park "Jigme Dorji National Park"), tigers were found to inhabit the deeper parts of forests while the smaller predators were pushed closer to the fringes.[[140]](#cite_note-140)

### Reproduction and life cycle

"Tiger cub" redirects here. For other uses, see [Tiger Cub](/wiki/Tiger_Cub_(disambiguation) "Tiger Cub (disambiguation)").

[![Tiger with cubs](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Tigress_with_cubs_in_Kanha_Tiger_reserve.jpg/250px-Tigress_with_cubs_in_Kanha_Tiger_reserve.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tigress_with_cubs_in_Kanha_Tiger_reserve.jpg)

A Bengal tiger family in Kanha Tiger Reserve

The tiger generally [mates](/wiki/Mating "Mating") all year round, particularly between November and April. A tigress is in oestrus for three to six days at a time, separated by three to nine week intervals.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47) A resident male mates with all the females within his home range, who signal their receptiveness by roaring and marking.[[141]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200442-141)[[142]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004145-142) Younger, transient males are also attracted, leading to a fight in which the more dominant, resident male drives the usurper off.[[143]](#cite_note-Sankhala-143)[[141]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200442-141) During courtship, the male is cautious with the female until she shows readiness to mate by positioning herself in [lordosis](/wiki/Lordosis_behavior "Lordosis behavior") with her tail to the side. [Copulation](/wiki/Copulation_(zoology) "Copulation (zoology)") typically lasts no more than 20 seconds, with the male biting the female by the scruff of her neck. After it is finished, the male quickly pulls away as the female may turn and slap him.[[141]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200442-141) Tiger pairs may stay together for up to four days and mate multiple times.[[144]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004148-144)[Gestation](/wiki/Gestation "Gestation") lasts around or over three months.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)

A tigress gives birth in a secluded location, be it in dense vegetation, in a cave or under a rocky shelter.[[145]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200445-145)[Litters](/wiki/Litter_(zoology) "Litter (zoology)") consist of as many as seven cubs, but two or three are more typical.[[143]](#cite_note-Sankhala-143)[[145]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200445-145) Newborn cubs weigh 785â€“1,610Â g (27.7â€“56.8Â oz) and are blind and [altricial](/wiki/Precociality_and_altriciality "Precociality and altriciality").[[145]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200445-145) The mother licks and cleans her cubs, suckles them and viciously defends them from any potential threat.[[143]](#cite_note-Sankhala-143) Cubs open their eyes at the age of three to 14 days and their vision becomes clear after a few more weeks.[[145]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200445-145) They can leave the denning site after two months and around the same time they start eating meat.[[143]](#cite_note-Sankhala-143)[[146]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200450%E2%80%9351-146) The mother only leaves them alone to hunt and even then she does not travel far.[[147]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200450-147) When she suspects an area is no longer safe, she moves her cubs to a new spot, transporting them one by one by grabbing them by the scruff of the neck with her mouth.[[148]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200451-148)
A tigress in Sikhote-Alin Biosphere Reserve maximised the time spent with her cubs by reducing her home range, killing larger prey and returning to her den more rapidly than without cubs; when the cubs started to eat meat, she took them to kill sites, thereby optimising their protection and access to food.[[149]](#cite_note-149)
In the same reserve, one of 21 cubs died in over eight years of monitoring and mortality did not differ between male and female juveniles.[[150]](#cite_note-150)
Tiger monitoring over six years in [Ranthambore Tiger Reserve](/wiki/Ranthambore_Tiger_Reserve "Ranthambore Tiger Reserve") indicated an average annual survival rate of around 85 percent for 74 male and female cubs; survival rate increased to 97 percent for both males and female juveniles of one to two years of age.[[151]](#cite_note-Sadhu2017-151)
Causes of cub mortality include predators, floods, fires, death of the mother and fatal injuries.[[148]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200451-148)[[152]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200451-152)[[153]](#cite_note-Barlow2009-153)[[151]](#cite_note-Sadhu2017-151)

[![Tigress with cub in snow](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Panthera_tigris_altaica_13_-_Buffalo_Zoo.jpg/250px-Panthera_tigris_altaica_13_-_Buffalo_Zoo.jpg)](/wiki/File:Panthera_tigris_altaica_13_-_Buffalo_Zoo.jpg)

A Siberian tigress with her cub at [Buffalo Zoo](/wiki/Buffalo_Zoo "Buffalo Zoo")

After around two months, the cubs are able to follow their mother. They still hide in vegetation when she goes hunting. Young bond through play fighting and practice stalking. A hierarchy develops in the litter, with the biggest cub, often a male, being the most dominant and the first to eat its fill at a kill.[[154]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200461,_66%E2%80%9367-154) Around the age of six months, cubs are fully weaned and have more freedom to explore their environment. Between eight and ten months, they accompany their mother on hunts.[[117]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200463-117) A cub can make a kill as early as 11 months and reach independence as a juvenile of 18 to 24 months of age; males become independent earlier than females.[[155]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaller1967270,_276-155)[[151]](#cite_note-Sadhu2017-151) Radio-collared tigers in Chitwan started leaving their natal areas at the age of 19 months.[[89]](#cite_note-Smith1993-89) Young females are [sexually mature](/wiki/Sexual_maturity "Sexual maturity") at three to four years, whereas males are at four to five years.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[Generation length](/wiki/Generation_time "Generation time") of the tiger is about 7â€“10 years.[[156]](#cite_note-156)
Wild Bengal tigers live 12â€“15 years.[[157]](#cite_note-157) Data from the International Tiger [Studbook](/wiki/Studbook "Studbook") 1938â€“2018 indicate that captive tigers lived up to 19 years.[[158]](#cite_note-158)

The father does not play a role in raising the young, but he encounters and interacts with them. The resident male appears to visit the femaleâ€“cub families within his home range. They socialise and even share kills.[[159]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200459,_89-159)[[160]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200455%E2%80%9356-160) One male was recorded looking after cubs whose mother had died.[[161]](#cite_note-161) By defending his home range, the male protects the females and cubs from other males.[[162]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills200459-162) When a new male takes over, dependent cubs are at risk of [infanticide](/wiki/Infanticide_(zoology) "Infanticide (zoology)") as the male attempts to sire his own young with the females.[[163]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar200466-163) A seven-year long study in Chitwan National Park revealed that 12 of 56 detected cubs and juveniles were killed by new males taking over home ranges.[[153]](#cite_note-Barlow2009-153)

### Health and diseases

Tigers are recorded as hosts for various parasites including tapeworms like *[Diphyllobothrium](/wiki/Diphyllobothrium "Diphyllobothrium") erinacei*, *[Taenia pisiformis](/wiki/Taenia_pisiformis "Taenia pisiformis")* in India and [nematodes](/wiki/Nematode "Nematode") like *[Toxocara](/wiki/Toxocara "Toxocara")* species in India and *[Physaloptera](/wiki/Physaloptera "Physaloptera") preputialis*, *[Dirofilaria](/wiki/Dirofilaria "Dirofilaria") ursi* and *Uiteinarta* species in Siberia.[[47]](#cite_note-Mazak1981-47)[Canine distemper](/wiki/Canine_distemper "Canine distemper") is known to occur in Siberian tigers.[[164]](#cite_note-164) A [morbillivirus](/wiki/Morbillivirus "Morbillivirus") infection was the likely cause of death of a tigress in the Russian Far East that was also tested positive for [feline panleukopenia](/wiki/Feline_panleukopenia "Feline panleukopenia") and [feline coronavirus](/wiki/Feline_coronavirus "Feline coronavirus").[[165]](#cite_note-165)
Blood samples from 11 adult tigers in Nepal showed [antibodies](/wiki/Antibody "Antibody") for [canine parvovirus](/wiki/Canine_parvovirus "Canine parvovirus")-2, [feline herpesvirus](/wiki/Feline_herpesvirus "Feline herpesvirus"), feline coronavirus, [leptospirosis](/wiki/Leptospirosis "Leptospirosis") and *[Toxoplasma gondii](/wiki/Toxoplasma_gondii "Toxoplasma gondii")*.[[166]](#cite_note-166)

Threats
-------

The tiger has been listed as [Endangered](/wiki/Endangered "Endangered") on the [IUCN Red List](/wiki/IUCN_Red_List "IUCN Red List") since 1986 and the global tiger population is thought to have continuously declined from an estimated population of 5,000â€“8,262 tigers in the late 1990s to 3,726â€“5,578 individuals estimated as of 2022.[[1]](#cite_note-iucn-1) During 2001â€“2020, landscapes where tigers live declined from 1,025,488Â km2 (395,943Â sqÂ mi) to 911,901Â km2 (352,087Â sqÂ mi).[[65]](#cite_note-Sanderson_al2023-65)[Habitat destruction](/wiki/Habitat_destruction "Habitat destruction"), [habitat fragmentation](/wiki/Habitat_fragmentation "Habitat fragmentation") and [poaching](/wiki/Poaching "Poaching") for fur and body parts are the major threats that contributed to the decrease of tiger populations in all range countries.[[1]](#cite_note-iucn-1)

Protected areas in central India are highly fragmented due to linear infrastructure like roads, railway lines, [transmission lines](/wiki/Transmission_line "Transmission line"), [irrigation](/wiki/Irrigation "Irrigation") channels and [mining](/wiki/Mining "Mining") activities in their vicinity.[[167]](#cite_note-167)
In the [Tanintharyi Region](/wiki/Tanintharyi_Region "Tanintharyi Region") of southern Myanmar, [deforestation](/wiki/Deforestation "Deforestation") coupled with mining activities and high hunting pressure threatens the tiger population.[[168]](#cite_note-168)
In Thailand, nine of 15 protected areas hosting tigers are isolated and fragmented, offering a low probability for dispersal between them; four of these have not harboured tigers since about 2013.[[169]](#cite_note-169)
In Peninsular Malaysia, 8,315.7Â km2 (3,210.7Â sqÂ mi) of tiger habitat was cleared during 1988â€“2012, most of it for industrial [plantations](/wiki/Plantation "Plantation").[[170]](#cite_note-170)
Large-scale land acquisitions of about 23,000Â km2 (8,900Â sqÂ mi) for commercial [agriculture](/wiki/Agriculture "Agriculture") and timber extraction in Cambodia contributed to the fragmentation of potential tiger habitat, especially in the Eastern Plains.[[171]](#cite_note-171)[Inbreeding depression](/wiki/Inbreeding_depression "Inbreeding depression") coupled with habitat destruction, insufficient prey resources and poaching is a threat to the small and isolated tiger population in the [Changbai Mountains](/wiki/Changbai_Mountains "Changbai Mountains") along the Chinaâ€“Russia border.[[172]](#cite_note-172)
In China, tigers became the target of large-scale 'anti-pest' campaigns in the early 1950s, where suitable habitats were fragmented following deforestation and resettlement of people to rural areas, who hunted tigers and prey species. Though tiger hunting was prohibited in 1977, the population continued to decline and is considered extinct in [South China](/wiki/South_China "South China") since 2001.[[173]](#cite_note-173)[[174]](#cite_note-174)

[![Tiger rug displayed on wall behind a man with a gun](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_jager_poseert_bij_de_huid_van_een_geschoten_tijger_bij_Kalitapakdoewoer_TMnr_10024166.jpg/250px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_jager_poseert_bij_de_huid_van_een_geschoten_tijger_bij_Kalitapakdoewoer_TMnr_10024166.jpg)](/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Een_jager_poseert_bij_de_huid_van_een_geschoten_tijger_bij_Kalitapakdoewoer_TMnr_10024166.jpg)

A [Javan tiger](/wiki/Javan_tiger "Javan tiger") skin, 1915

Tiger populations in India have been targeted by poachers since the 1990s and were extirpated in two tiger reserves in 2005 and 2009.[[175]](#cite_note-Jhala_al2021-175)
Between March 2017 and January 2020, 630 activities of hunters using [snares](/wiki/Trapping#Snares "Trapping"), drift nets, hunting platforms and hunting dogs were discovered in a reserve forest of about 1,000Â km2 (390Â sqÂ mi) in southern Myanmar.[[176]](#cite_note-176)[Nam Et-Phou Louey National Park](/wiki/Nam_Et-Phou_Louey_National_Park "Nam Et-Phou Louey National Park") was considered the last important site for the tiger in Laos, but it has not been recorded there at least since 2013; this population likely fell victim to indiscriminate snaring.[[177]](#cite_note-177)[Anti-poaching](/wiki/Anti-poaching "Anti-poaching") units in Sumatra's [Kerinci Seblat](/wiki/Kerinci_Seblat_National_Park "Kerinci Seblat National Park") landscape removed 362 tiger snare traps and seized 91 tiger skins during 2005â€“2016; annual poaching rates increased with rising skin prices.[[178]](#cite_note-178)
Poaching is also the main threat to the tiger population in far eastern Russia, where [logging](/wiki/Logging "Logging") roads facilitate access for poachers and people harvesting forest products that are important for prey species to survive in winter.[[179]](#cite_note-179)

Body parts of 207 tigers were detected during 21 surveys in 1991â€“2014 in two wildlife markets in Myanmar catering to customers in Thailand and China.[[180]](#cite_note-180)
During the years 2000â€“2022, at least 3,377 tigers were [confiscated](/wiki/Confiscation "Confiscation") in 2,205 seizures in 28 countries; seizures encompassed 665 live and 654 dead individuals, 1,313 whole tiger skins, 16,214 body parts like bones, teeth, paws, claws, whiskers and 1.1Â t (1.1 long tons; 1.2 short tons) of meat; 759 seizures in India encompassed body parts of 893 tigers; and 403 seizures in Thailand involved mostly captive-bred tigers.[[181]](#cite_note-181) Seizures in Nepal between January 2011 and December 2015 obtained 585 pieces of tiger body parts and two whole carcasses in 19 districts.[[182]](#cite_note-182) Seizure data from India during 2001â€“2021 indicate that tiger skins were the most often traded body parts, followed by claws, bones and teeth; trafficking routes mainly passed through the states of Maharashtra, Karnataka, Tamil Nadu and Assam.[[183]](#cite_note-183)
A total of 292 illegal tiger parts were confiscated at US ports of entry from personal baggage, air cargo and mail between 2003 and 2012.[[184]](#cite_note-184)

Demand for tiger parts for use in [traditional Chinese medicine](/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine "Traditional Chinese medicine") has also been cited as a major threat to tiger populations.[[185]](#cite_note-185)
Interviews with local people in the Bangladeshi Sundarbans revealed that they kill tigers for local consumption and trade of skins, bones and meat, in [retaliation](/wiki/Retaliation "Retaliation") for attacks by tigers and for excitement.[[186]](#cite_note-186)
Tiger body parts like skins, bones, teeth and hair are consumed locally by wealthy Bangladeshis and are illegally trafficked from Bangladesh to 15 countries including India, China, Malaysia, Korea, Vietnam, Cambodia, Japan and the United Kingdom via land borders, airports and seaports.[[187]](#cite_note-Uddin2023-187)
Tiger bone glue is the prevailing tiger product purchased for medicinal purposes in [Hanoi](/wiki/Hanoi "Hanoi") and [Ho Chi Minh City](/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh_City "Ho Chi Minh City").[[188]](#cite_note-188) "Tiger farm" facilities in China and Southeast Asia breed tigers for their parts, but these appear to make the threat to wild populations worse by increasing the demand for tiger products.[[189]](#cite_note-Worldwildlife-189)

Local people killing tigers in retaliation for attacking and preying on livestock is a threat in several tiger range countries, as this consequence of [humanâ€“wildlife conflict](/wiki/Human%E2%80%93wildlife_conflict "Humanâ€“wildlife conflict") also contributes to the decline of the population.[[190]](#cite_note-190)[[191]](#cite_note-191)[[192]](#cite_note-192)[[193]](#cite_note-193)[[194]](#cite_note-194)

Conservation
------------

Main article: [Tiger conservation](/wiki/Tiger_conservation "Tiger conservation")

Further information: [21st Century Tiger](/wiki/21st_Century_Tiger "21st Century Tiger")

Global wild tiger population

| Country | Year | Estimate |
| --- | --- | --- |
| [India](/wiki/India "India") [India](/wiki/India "India") | 2022 | 3,167â€“3,682[[195]](#cite_note-Qureshi2023-195) |
| [Russia](/wiki/Russia "Russia") [Russia](/wiki/Russia "Russia") | 2022 | 573â€“600[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196) |
| [Indonesia](/wiki/Indonesia "Indonesia") [Indonesia](/wiki/Indonesia "Indonesia") | 2022 | 393[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196) |
| [Nepal](/wiki/Nepal "Nepal") [Nepal](/wiki/Nepal "Nepal") | 2022 | 316â€“355[[197]](#cite_note-197) |
| [Thailand](/wiki/Thailand "Thailand") [Thailand](/wiki/Thailand "Thailand") | 2022 | 148â€“189[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196) |
| [Malaysia](/wiki/Malaysia "Malaysia") [Malaysia](/wiki/Malaysia "Malaysia") | 2022 | <150[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196) |
| [Bhutan](/wiki/Bhutan "Bhutan") [Bhutan](/wiki/Bhutan "Bhutan") | 2022 | 131[[198]](#cite_note-198) |
| [Bangladesh](/wiki/Bangladesh "Bangladesh") [Bangladesh](/wiki/Bangladesh "Bangladesh") | 2022 | 118â€“122[[199]](#cite_note-199) |
| [China](/wiki/China "China") [China](/wiki/China "China") | 2022 | >60[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196) |
| [Myanmar](/wiki/Myanmar "Myanmar") [Myanmar](/wiki/Myanmar "Myanmar") | 2022 | 28[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196) |
| **Total** |  | **5,638â€“5,899** |

Internationally, the tiger is protected under [CITES Appendix I](/wiki/CITES#Appendix_I "CITES"), banning trade of live tigers and their body parts.[[1]](#cite_note-iucn-1)
In [Russia](/wiki/Russia "Russia"), hunting the tiger has been banned since 1952.[[200]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESludskii1992202-200)
In [Bhutan](/wiki/Bhutan "Bhutan"), it has been protected since 1969 and enlisted as totally protected since 1995.[[201]](#cite_note-Tandin_al2018-201) Since 1972, it has been afforded the highest protection level under India's [Wild Life (Protection) Act, 1972](/wiki/Wild_Life_(Protection)_Act,_1972 "Wild Life (Protection) Act, 1972").[[202]](#cite_note-Aryal-202)
In [Nepal](/wiki/Nepal "Nepal") and [Bangladesh](/wiki/Bangladesh "Bangladesh"), it has been protected since 1973.[[202]](#cite_note-Aryal-202)[[187]](#cite_note-Uddin2023-187)
Since 1976, it has been totally protected under [Malaysia](/wiki/Malaysia "Malaysia")'s Protection of Wild Life Act,[[203]](#cite_note-203) and the country's Wildlife Conservation Act enacted in 2010 increased punishments for wildlife-related crimes.[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196)
In [Indonesia](/wiki/Indonesia "Indonesia"), it has been protected since 1990.[[204]](#cite_note-204)
In [China](/wiki/China "China"), the trade in tiger body parts was banned in 1993.[[205]](#cite_note-205)
The [Thai](/wiki/Thailand "Thailand") Wildlife Preservation and Protection Act was enacted in 2019 to combat poaching and trading of body parts.[[206]](#cite_note-future-206)

In 1973, the [National Tiger Conservation Authority](/wiki/National_Tiger_Conservation_Authority "National Tiger Conservation Authority") and [Project Tiger](/wiki/Project_Tiger "Project Tiger") were founded in [India](/wiki/India "India") to gain public support for tiger conservation.[[175]](#cite_note-Jhala_al2021-175) Since then, 53 [tiger reserves](/wiki/Tiger_reserves_of_India "Tiger reserves of India") covering an area of 75,796Â km2 (29,265Â sqÂ mi) have been established in the country up to 2022.[[195]](#cite_note-Qureshi2023-195) These efforts contributed to the recovery of India's tiger population between 2006 and 2018 so that it occurs in an area of about 138,200Â km2 (53,400Â sqÂ mi).[[207]](#cite_note-207)

[Myanmar](/wiki/Myanmar "Myanmar")'s national tiger conservation strategy developed in 2003 comprises management tasks such as restoration of degraded habitats, increasing the extent of protected areas and wildlife corridors, protecting tiger prey species, thwarting tiger killing and illegal trade of its body parts and promoting public awareness through wildlife education programmes.[[208]](#cite_note-208)
Bhutan's first Tiger Action Plan implemented during 2006â€“2015 revolved around habitat conservation, humanâ€“wildlife conflict management, education and awareness; the second Action Plan aimed at increasing the country's tiger population by 20% until 2023 compared to 2015.[[201]](#cite_note-Tandin_al2018-201)
In 2009, the Bangladesh Tiger Action Plan was initiated to stabilise the country's tiger population, maintain habitat and a sufficient prey base, improve law enforcement and foster cooperation between governmental agencies responsible for tiger conservation.[[209]](#cite_note-209)
The Thailand Tiger Action Plan [ratified](/wiki/Ratified "Ratified") in 2010 envisioned increasing the country's tiger populations by 50% in the [Western Forest Complex](/wiki/Western_Forest_Complex "Western Forest Complex") and [Dong Phayayenâ€“Khao Yai Forest Complex](/wiki/Dong_Phayayen%E2%80%93Khao_Yai_Forest_Complex "Dong Phayayenâ€“Khao Yai Forest Complex") and reestablish populations in three potential landscapes until 2022.[[210]](#cite_note-210)
The Indonesian National Tiger Recovery Program ratified in 2010 aimed at increasing the Sumatran tiger population by 2022.[[211]](#cite_note-211) The third strategic and action plan for the conservation of the Sumatran tiger for the years 2020â€“2030 revolves around strengthening management of small tiger population units of less than 20 mature individuals and connectivity between 13 forest patches in [North Sumatra](/wiki/North_Sumatra "North Sumatra") and [West Sumatra](/wiki/West_Sumatra "West Sumatra") provinces.[[212]](#cite_note-212)

[![Night shot of a tiger face close to the camera](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Wild_Sumatran_tiger.jpg/250px-Wild_Sumatran_tiger.jpg)](/wiki/File:Wild_Sumatran_tiger.jpg)

Wild Sumatran tiger caught by camera trap

Increases in anti-poaching patrol efforts in four Russian protected areas during 2011â€“2014 contributed to reducing poaching, stabilising the tiger population and improving protection of ungulate populations.[[213]](#cite_note-213) Poaching and trafficking were declared to be moderate and serious crimes in 2019.[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196)
Anti-poaching operations were also established in Nepal in 2010, with increased cooperation and intelligence sharing between agencies. These policies have led to many years of "zero poaching" and the country's tiger population has doubled in a decade.[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196)
Anti-poaching patrols in the 1,200Â km2 (460Â sqÂ mi) large core area of [Taman Negara](/wiki/Taman_Negara "Taman Negara") lead to a decrease of poaching frequency from 34 detected incidents in 2015â€“2016 to 20 incidents during 2018â€“2019; the arrest of seven poaching teams and removal of snares facilitated the survival of three resident female tigers and at least 11 cubs.[[214]](#cite_note-214)
Army and police officers are deployed for patrolling together with staff of protected areas in Malaysia.[[196]](#cite_note-globaltiger-196)

[Wildlife corridors](/wiki/Wildlife_corridor "Wildlife corridor") are important conservation measures as they facilitate tiger populations to connect between protected areas; tigers use at least nine corridors that were established in the [Terai Arc Landscape](/wiki/Terai_Arc_Landscape "Terai Arc Landscape") and [Sivalik Hills](/wiki/Sivalik_Hills "Sivalik Hills") in both Nepal and India.[[215]](#cite_note-215)
Corridors in forested areas with low human encroachment are highly suitable.[[216]](#cite_note-216)[[217]](#cite_note-217)
In West Sumatra, 12 wildlife corridors were identified as high priority for mitigating humanâ€“wildlife conflicts.[[218]](#cite_note-218)
In 2019, China and Russia signed a [memorandum of understanding](/wiki/Memorandum_of_understanding "Memorandum of understanding") for transboundary cooperation between two protected areas, [Northeast China Tiger and Leopard National Park](/wiki/Northeast_China_Tiger_and_Leopard_National_Park "Northeast China Tiger and Leopard National Park") and [Land of the Leopard National Park](/wiki/Land_of_the_Leopard_National_Park "Land of the Leopard National Park"), that includes the creation of wildlife corridors and bilateral monitoring and patrolling along the Sino-Russian border.[[219]](#cite_note-219)

Rescued and [rehabilitated](/wiki/Wildlife_rehabilitation "Wildlife rehabilitation") problem tigers and [orphaned](/wiki/Orphan "Orphan") tiger cubs have been released into the wild and monitored in India, Sumatra and Russia.[[84]](#cite_note-Sarkar2016-84)[[220]](#cite_note-Priatna2012-220)[[221]](#cite_note-221)
In Kazakhstan, [habitat restoration](/wiki/Ecological_restoration "Ecological restoration") and [reintroduction](/wiki/Species_reintroduction "Species reintroduction") of prey species in [Ile-Balkash Nature Reserve](https://kk.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%86%D0%BB%D0%B5-%D0%91%D0%B0%D0%BB%D2%9B%D0%B0%D1%88_(%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B5%D1%80%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%82) "kk:Ğ†Ğ»Ğµ-Ğ‘Ğ°Ğ»Ò›Ğ°Ñˆ (Ñ€ĞµĞ·ĞµÑ€Ğ²Ğ°Ñ‚)") have progressed and tiger reintroduction is planned for 2025.[[222]](#cite_note-222)
Reintroduction of tigers is considered possible in eastern Cambodia, once management of protected areas is improved and forest loss stabilized.[[223]](#cite_note-223) South China tigers are kept and bred in Chinese zoos, with plans to reintroduce their offspring into remote protected areas.[[15]](#cite_note-Wang2023-15)[[116]](#cite_note-F%C3%A0bregas-116) Coordinated [breeding programs](/wiki/Breeding_program "Breeding program") among [zoos](/wiki/Zoo "Zoo") have led to enough genetic diversity in tigers to act as "insurance against extinction in the wild".[[224]](#cite_note-Lou2008-224)

Relationship with humans
------------------------

[![Painting of people hunting tigers on elephant-back](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ElephantbackTigerHunt.jpg/250px-ElephantbackTigerHunt.jpg)](/wiki/File:ElephantbackTigerHunt.jpg)

Tiger hunting on elephant-back in India, 1808

### Hunting

Main article: [Tiger hunting](/wiki/Tiger_hunting "Tiger hunting")

Tigers have been hunted by humans for millennia, as indicated by a painting on the [Bhimbetka rock shelters](/wiki/Bhimbetka_rock_shelters "Bhimbetka rock shelters") in India that is dated to 5,000â€“6,000 years ago. They were hunted throughout their range in Asia, chased on horseback, elephant-back or even with sled dogs and killed with spears and later firearms. Such hunts were conducted both by Asian governments and empires like the [Mughal Empire](/wiki/Mughal_Empire "Mughal Empire"), as well as European colonists. Tigers were often hunted as [trophies](/wiki/Trophy_hunting "Trophy hunting") and because of their perceived danger.[[225]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004187%E2%80%93200-225) An estimated 80,000 tigers were killed between 1875 and 1925.[[226]](#cite_note-Tiger-hunting-226)[[227]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004193-227)

### Attacks

Main article: [Tiger attack](/wiki/Tiger_attack "Tiger attack")

[![Tiger standing along the banks of a mangrove swamp](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Sundarban_Tiger.jpg/250px-Sundarban_Tiger.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sundarban_Tiger.jpg)

A Bengal tiger in the [Sundarbans](/wiki/Sundarban "Sundarban")

In most areas, tigers avoid humans, but attacks are a risk wherever people coexist with them.[[228]](#cite_note-conflict-228)[[229]](#cite_note-Goodrich2010-229) Dangerous encounters are more likely to occur in [edge](/wiki/Edge_effects "Edge effects") habitats between wild and agricultural areas.[[228]](#cite_note-conflict-228) Most attacks on humans are defensive, including protection of young; however, tigers do sometimes see people as prey.[[229]](#cite_note-Goodrich2010-229)[Man-eating](/wiki/Man-eating_animal#Tigers "Man-eating animal") tigers tend to be old and disabled.[[57]](#cite_note-Miquelle-57) Tigers driven from their home ranges are also at risk of turning to man-eating.[[230]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills2004108%E2%80%93110-230)

At the beginning of the 20th century, the [Champawat Tiger](/wiki/Champawat_Tiger "Champawat Tiger") was responsible for over 430 human deaths in Nepal and India before she was shot by [Jim Corbett](/wiki/Jim_Corbett "Jim Corbett").[[231]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004276-231) This tigress suffered from broken teeth and was unable to kill normal prey. Modern authors speculate that sustaining on meagre human flesh forced the cat to kill more and more.[[232]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200673%E2%80%9374-232) Tiger attacks were particularly high in Singapore during the mid-19th century, when plantations expanded into the tiger's habitat.[[233]](#cite_note-233) In the 1840s, the number of deaths in the area ranged from 200 to 300 annually.[[234]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004274-234)[Tiger attacks in the Sundarbans](/wiki/Tiger_attacks_in_the_Sundarbans "Tiger attacks in the Sundarbans") caused 1,396 human deaths in the period 1935â€“2006 according to official records of the [Bangladesh Forest Department](/wiki/Forest_Department_(Bangladesh) "Forest Department (Bangladesh)").[[235]](#cite_note-235) Victims of these attacks are local villagers who enter the tiger's domain to collect resources like wood and honey. Fishermen have been particularly common targets. Methods to counter tiger attacks have included face masks worn backwards, protective clothes, sticks and carefully stationed electric dummies.[[236]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMills2004111%E2%80%93113-236)

### Captivity

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Clean_Toes_are_a_Tiger%27s_Friend_%2815588882074%29.jpg/250px-Clean_Toes_are_a_Tiger%27s_Friend_%2815588882074%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Clean_Toes_are_a_Tiger%27s_Friend_(15588882074).jpg)

Tiger at [Big Cat Rescue](/wiki/Big_Cat_Rescue "Big Cat Rescue") in 2014

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Ringling_Bros_and_Barnum_%26_Bailey_Circus_Gunther_Gebel-Williams_1969.jpg/250px-Ringling_Bros_and_Barnum_%26_Bailey_Circus_Gunther_Gebel-Williams_1969.jpg)](/wiki/File:Ringling_Bros_and_Barnum_%26_Bailey_Circus_Gunther_Gebel-Williams_1969.jpg)

Publicity photo of animal trainer [Gunther Gebel-Williams](/wiki/Gunther_Gebel-Williams "Gunther Gebel-Williams") with several of his trained tigers, c. 1969

Tigers have been kept in captivity since ancient times. In [ancient Rome](/wiki/Ancient_Rome "Ancient Rome"), tigers were displayed in [amphitheatres](/wiki/Amphitheatre "Amphitheatre"); they were slaughtered in [venatio](/wiki/Venatio "Venatio") hunts and used to [kill criminals](/wiki/Damnatio_ad_bestias "Damnatio ad bestias").[[237]](#cite_note-237) The Mongol ruler [Kublai Khan](/wiki/Kublai_Khan "Kublai Khan") is reported to have kept tigers in the 13th century. Starting in the [Middle Ages](/wiki/Middle_Ages "Middle Ages"), tigers were being kept in European [menageries](/wiki/Menagerie "Menagerie").[[238]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004179%E2%80%93180-238) Tigers and other exotic animals were mainly used for the entertainment of elites but from the 19th century onward, they were exhibited more to the public. Tigers were particularly big attractions and their captive population soared.[[239]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2006126%E2%80%93130-239) In 2020, there were over 8,000 captive tigers in Asia, over 5,000 in the US and no less than 850 in Europe.[[240]](#cite_note-240) There are more tigers in captivity than in the wild.[[224]](#cite_note-Lou2008-224) Captive tigers may display [stereotypical behaviours](/wiki/Stereotypy_(non-human) "Stereotypy (non-human)") such as pacing or inactivity. Modern zoos are able to reduce such behaviours with exhibits designed so the animals can move between separate but connected enclosures.[[241]](#cite_note-241) Enrichment items are also important for the cat's welfare and the stimulation of its natural behaviours.[[242]](#cite_note-242)

Tigers have played prominent roles in [circuses](/wiki/Circus "Circus") and other live performances. [Ringling Bros](/wiki/Ringling_Bros._and_Barnum_%26_Bailey_Circus "Ringling Bros. and Barnum & Bailey Circus") included many tiger tamers in the 20th century including [Mabel Stark](/wiki/Mabel_Stark "Mabel Stark"), who became a big draw and had a long career. She was well known for being able to control the tigers despite being a small woman; using "manly" tools like whips and guns. Another trainer was [Clyde Beatty](/wiki/Clyde_Beatty "Clyde Beatty"), who used chairs, whips and guns to provoke tigers and other beasts into acting fierce and allowed him to appear courageous. He would perform with as many as 40 tigers and lions in one act. From the 1960s onward, trainers like [Gunther Gebel-Williams](/wiki/Gunther_Gebel-Williams "Gunther Gebel-Williams") would use gentler methods to control their animals. [Sara Houcke](/wiki/Sara_Houcke "Sara Houcke") was dubbed "the Tiger Whisperer" as she trained the cats to obey her by whispering to them.[[243]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004202%E2%80%93204-243)[Siegfried & Roy](/wiki/Siegfried_%26_Roy "Siegfried & Roy") became famous for performing with white tigers in [Las Vegas](/wiki/Las_Vegas "Las Vegas"). The act ended in 2003 when a tiger attacked Roy during a performance.[[244]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2006140%E2%80%93141-244) In 2009, tigers were the most traded circus animals.[[245]](#cite_note-245) The use of tigers and other animals in shows eventually declined in many countries due to pressure from [animal rights](/wiki/Animal_rights "Animal rights") groups and greater desires from the public to see them in more natural settings. Several countries restrict or ban such acts.[[246]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004204%E2%80%93205-246)

Tigers have become popular in the [exotic pet](/wiki/Exotic_pet "Exotic pet") trade, particularly in the United States[[247]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004214-247) where only 6% of the captive tiger population in 2020 were being housed in zoos and other facilities approved by the [Association of Zoos and Aquariums](/wiki/Association_of_Zoos_and_Aquariums "Association of Zoos and Aquariums").[[189]](#cite_note-Worldwildlife-189) Private collectors are thought to be ill-equipped to provide proper care for tigers, which compromises their welfare. They can also threaten public safety by allowing people to interact with them.[[189]](#cite_note-Worldwildlife-189) The keeping of tigers and other big cats by private people was banned in the US in 2022.[[248]](#cite_note-248) Most countries in the European Union have banned breeding and keeping tigers outside of licensed zoos and rescue centres, but some still allow private holdings.[[249]](#cite_note-249)

### Cultural significance

Main article: [Cultural depictions of tigers](/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_tigers "Cultural depictions of tigers")

Further information: [Tiger worship](/wiki/Tiger_worship "Tiger worship")

[![Badge of black tiger with golden stripes](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Bronze_Tiger_Tally_%22Jie%22_with_Gold_Inlay_from_Tomb_of_Zhao_Mo.jpg/250px-Bronze_Tiger_Tally_%22Jie%22_with_Gold_Inlay_from_Tomb_of_Zhao_Mo.jpg)](/wiki/File:Bronze_Tiger_Tally_%22Jie%22_with_Gold_Inlay_from_Tomb_of_Zhao_Mo.jpg)

Tiger-shaped *jie* (badge of authority) with gold inlays, from the tomb of [Zhao Mo](/wiki/Zhao_Mo "Zhao Mo")

The tiger is among the most famous of the [charismatic megafauna](/wiki/Charismatic_megafauna "Charismatic megafauna"). [Kailash Sankhala](/wiki/Kailash_Sankhala "Kailash Sankhala") has called it "a rare combination of courage, ferocity and brilliant colour",[[143]](#cite_note-Sankhala-143) while Candy d'Sa calls it "fierce and commanding on the outside, but noble and discerning on the inside". In a 2004 online poll involving more than 50,000 people from 73 countries, the tiger was voted the world's favourite animal with 21% of the vote, narrowly beating the dog.[[250]](#cite_note-250) Similarly, a 2018 study found the tiger to be the most popular wild animal based on surveys, as well as appearances on websites of major zoos and posters of some animated movies.[[251]](#cite_note-251)

While the lion represented royalty and power in [Western culture](/wiki/Western_culture "Western culture"), the tiger played such a role in various [Asian cultures](/wiki/Culture_of_Asia "Culture of Asia"). In [ancient China](/wiki/Ancient_China "Ancient China"), the tiger was seen as the "king of the forest" and symbolised the power of the [emperor](/wiki/Emperor_of_China "Emperor of China").[[252]](#cite_note-Symbolism-252) In [Chinese astrology](/wiki/Chinese_astrology "Chinese astrology"), the tiger is the third out of 12 symbols in the [Chinese zodiac](/wiki/Chinese_zodiac "Chinese zodiac") and controls the period between 15:00 and 17:00 o'clock in the afternoon. The [Year of the Tiger](/wiki/Tiger_(zodiac) "Tiger (zodiac)") is thought to bring "dramatic and extreme events". The [White Tiger](/wiki/White_Tiger_(mythology) "White Tiger (mythology)") is one of the [Four Symbols](/wiki/Four_Symbols "Four Symbols") of the [Chinese constellations](/wiki/Chinese_constellations "Chinese constellations"), representing the west along with the [yin](/wiki/Yin_and_yang "Yin and yang") and the season of autumn. It is the counterpart to the [Azure Dragon](/wiki/Azure_Dragon "Azure Dragon"), which conversely symbolises the east, yang and springtime.[[253]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200639,_46-253) The tiger is one of the animals displayed on the [Pashupati seal](/wiki/Pashupati_seal "Pashupati seal") of the [Indus Valley Civilisation](/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation "Indus Valley Civilisation"). The big cat was depicted on seals and coins during the [Chola dynasty](/wiki/Chola_dynasty "Chola dynasty") of southern India, as it was the official emblem.[[254]](#cite_note-254)

[![Painting of an eight-armed goddess riding a tiger biting a buffalo demon](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Durga_Mahisasuramardini.JPG/250px-Durga_Mahisasuramardini.JPG)](/wiki/File:Durga_Mahisasuramardini.JPG)

The Hindu goddess [Durga](/wiki/Durga "Durga") riding a tiger. [Guler](/wiki/Guler_State "Guler State") school, early 18th century

Tigers have had religious and folkloric significance. In [Buddhism](/wiki/Buddhism "Buddhism"), the tiger, monkey and deer are the Three Senseless Creatures, with the tiger symbolising anger.[[255]](#cite_note-Cooper92-255) In [Hinduism](/wiki/Hinduism "Hinduism"), the tiger is the [vehicle](/wiki/Vahana "Vahana") of [Durga](/wiki/Durga "Durga"), the goddess of feminine power and peace, whom the gods created to fight demons. Similarly, in the [Greco-Roman world](/wiki/Greco-Roman_world "Greco-Roman world"), the tiger was depicted being ridden by the god [Dionysus](/wiki/Dionysus "Dionysus"). In [Korean mythology](/wiki/Korean_mythology "Korean mythology"), tigers are messengers of the [Mountain Gods](/wiki/Mountain_God "Mountain God").[[256]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200660,_86%E2%80%9388,_96-256) In both [Chinese](/wiki/Chinese_culture "Chinese culture") and [Korean culture](/wiki/Korean_culture "Korean culture"), tigers are seen as protectors against evil spirits and their image was used to decorate homes, tombs and articles of clothing.[[252]](#cite_note-Symbolism-252)[[257]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200696-257)[[258]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004152-258) In the folklore of Malaysia and Indonesia, "tiger [shamans](/wiki/Shaman "Shaman")" heal the sick by invoking the big cat. People turning into tigers and the inverse has also been widespread; in particular [weretigers](/wiki/Werecat#Asia "Werecat") are people who could change into tigers and back again. The [Mnong people](/wiki/Mnong_people "Mnong people") of Indochina believed that tigers could [shapeshift](/wiki/Shapeshift "Shapeshift") into humans.[[258]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThapar2004152-258) Among some [indigenous peoples of Siberia](/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Siberia "Indigenous peoples of Siberia"), it was believed that men would seduce women by transforming into tigers.[[252]](#cite_note-Symbolism-252)

[William Blake](/wiki/William_Blake "William Blake")'s 1794 poem "[The Tyger](/wiki/The_Tyger "The Tyger")" portrays the animal as the duality of beauty and ferocity. It is the sister poem to "[The Lamb](/wiki/The_Lamb_(poem) "The Lamb (poem)")" in Blake's *[Songs of Innocence and of Experience](/wiki/Songs_of_Innocence_and_of_Experience "Songs of Innocence and of Experience")* and he ponders how God could create such different creatures. The tiger is featured in the mediaeval Chinese novel *[Water Margin](/wiki/Water_Margin "Water Margin")*, where the cat battles and is slain by the bandit [Wu Song](/wiki/Wu_Song "Wu Song"), while the tiger [Shere Khan](/wiki/Shere_Khan "Shere Khan") in [Rudyard Kipling](/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling "Rudyard Kipling")'s *[The Jungle Book](/wiki/The_Jungle_Book "The Jungle Book")* (1894) is the mortal enemy of the human protagonist [Mowgli](/wiki/Mowgli "Mowgli"). Friendly tame tigers have also existed in culture, notably [Tigger](/wiki/Tigger "Tigger"), the [Winnie-the-Pooh](/wiki/Winnie-the-Pooh "Winnie-the-Pooh") character and [Tony the Tiger](/wiki/Tony_the_Tiger "Tony the Tiger"), the [Kellogg's](/wiki/Kellogg%27s "Kellogg's") cereal mascot.[[259]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200672%E2%80%9373,_78,_125%E2%80%93127,_147%E2%80%93148-259)
